## Domain Knowledge
**Domain**: chemical kinetics
**Analysis**: The target dA_dt is the reaction rate, and features include time t and concentration A. This is a classic chemical kinetics problem where the rate law expresses the dependence of reaction rate on reactant concentration and possibly explicit time if the rate constant varies (e.g., catalyst deactivation).

**Common formula structures** (built-in generic + LLM domain-specific):
- Arrhenius equation: k = c0 * exp(c1 / T) (chemical reaction rate dependence on temperature)
- Coulomb's / Newton's inverse-square law: F = c0 / r**2 (gravitational or electrostatic force)
- Stefan-Boltzmann law: P = c0 * T**4 (blackbody radiation power vs temperature)
- Beer-Lambert law: I = c0 * exp(c1 * x) (light intensity attenuation through a medium)
- Michaelis-Menten kinetics: v = c0 * S / (S + c1) (enzyme-catalyzed reaction saturation)
- Hill equation: y = c0 * x**c1 / (x**c1 + c2) (cooperative binding, sigmoidal dose-response)
- Kepler's third law: T**2 = c0 * a**3 (orbital period vs semi-major axis)
- Newton's law of cooling: T = c0 + c1 * exp(c2 * t) (exponential thermal relaxation)
- Clausius-Clapeyron relation: log(P) = c0 + c1 / T (vapor pressure vs temperature)
- Langmuir adsorption isotherm: theta = c0 * P / (1 + c1 * P) (surface adsorption equilibrium)
- Freundlich isotherm: q = c0 * C**c1 (empirical adsorption on heterogeneous surface)
- Stokes' drag: F = c0 * r * v (viscous drag force on a sphere in fluid)
- Logistic growth: N = c0 / (1 + c1 * exp(c2 * t)) (population dynamics with carrying capacity)
- Planck-Wien approximation: I = c0 * f**3 * exp(c1 * f / T) (thermal radiation spectrum)
- Zero-order kinetics: dA_dt = -c0 (constant rate of consumption)
- First-order kinetics: dA_dt = -c0 * A (rate proportional to concentration)
- Second-order kinetics: dA_dt = -c0 * A**2 (rate proportional to square of concentration)
- n-th order power-law: dA_dt = -c0 * A**c1 (general reaction order)
- Reversible first-order (with constant product): dA_dt = c0 - c1 * A (forward and backward rates, approximate)
- Autocatalytic kinetics: dA_dt = -c0 * A * (c1 - A) (rate maximal at intermediate A, logistic-like)
- Substrate inhibition kinetics: dA_dt = -c0 * A / (c1 + A + c2 * A**2) (rate suppressed at high concentration due to inhibition)
- Langmuir-Hinshelwood bimolecular: dA_dt = -c0 * A**2 / (1 + c1 * A)**2 (dual-site surface reaction)
- Time-decaying rate constant (exponential deactivation): dA_dt = -c0 * A * exp(-c1 * t) (catalyst or enzyme deactivation)
- Time-decaying rate constant (power-law): dA_dt = -c0 * A * t**c1 (gradual change of effective rate)
- Combined parallel pathways: dA_dt = -c0 * A - c1 * A**c2 (sum of multiple reaction orders)
- Sigmoidal rate with baseline: dA_dt = -c0 * A**c1 / (c2 + A**c1) + c3 (cooperative effects with offset)

**Formula structure heuristic features**:
- Oscillation damping envelope → Exponential decay envelope f(x)*exp(-c*Abs(x)) for symmetric damped oscillation, Gaussian envelope f(x)*exp(-c*x**2) for localized wave packets
- Saturation/clipping effects → Rational fraction x/(1+x), tanh; for variables with alternating positive/negative in oscillatory systems, selectively apply Abs
- Growth/decay → Exponential growth exp(c*t), Logistic 1/(1+exp(-c*x)), power law x**c
- Threshold/activation behavior → Max(x-c, 0), piecewise functions
- Coupling/cross effects → Product terms between variables x*y, power-law coupling x**c1 * y**c2
- Mass action law: reaction rate is usually proportional to the product of reactant concentrations each raised to a stoichiometric coefficient (here only A so power-law in A).
- Explicit time-dependence in rate law indicates time-varying conditions such as catalyst deactivation, radiation decay, or temperature ramping; often appears as exp(-c*t) or t^c factor.
- Negative sign for dA_dt signifies consumption of species A; positive sign would indicate production from other species (not present here, only A).
- Saturation/inhibition kinetics: when rate reaches a maximum and then declines at high A, the denominator will include higher-order terms like A^2 (substrate inhibition) or rational saturation forms.
- Autocatalysis yields rate proportional to A*(K - A), producing a bell-shaped rate profile with a maximum at A = K/2, and is typical of self-amplifying reactions.
- Reversible reactions add a constant offset (c0) in the rate expression when the reverse product concentration is effectively constant.
- Parallel or complex reactions often require sums of several kinetic terms (e.g., first-order plus second-order or multiple power laws) to describe multi-pathway contributions.
- Enzyme-like saturation (Michaelis-Menten) is a specific rational form; more complex surface or inhibition models generalize this with additional denominator terms (e.g., (1 + K A)^n).
- The exponent on A (reaction order) indicates molecularity and can be fractional in heterogeneous or chain reactions, requiring power-law or rational forms.
